            \item Given\/ $q\colon a\eq{A}x_1$, transport evaluates to post-composition by\/~$p$:
            \[
                \fun{transport}^{E_1}(p,q)\eq{a\eq{A}x_2}q\ct p.
            \]
            \item Post-composition with $p$\/ is an equivalence\/ $(a\eq{A}x_1)\simeq(a\eq{A}x_2)$.
        \end{enumerate}
        
        \item Let\/ $E_2\colon A\to\univ$ be the type family\/ $E_2(x)\defeq x\eq{A}a$.
        \begin{enumerate}[i),font=\upshape]
            \item Given\/ $q\colon x_1\eq{A}a$, transport evaluates to pre-composition by\/ $p^{-1}$:
            \[
                \fun{transport}^{E_2}(p,q)\eq{x_2\eq{A}a}p^{-1}\ct q.
            \]
            
            \item Pre-composition by\/ $p^{-1}$ is an equivalence\/ $(x_1\eq{A}a)\simeq(x_2\eq{A}a)$.
        \end{enumerate} 
        
        \item Let\/ $E_3\colon A\to\univ$ be the type family\/ $E_3(x)\defeq x\eq{A}x$.
        \begin{enumerate}[i),font=\upshape]
            \item Given\/ $q\colon x_1\eq{A}x_1$, transport evaluates to conjugation by\/ $p$:
            \[
                \fun{transport}^{E_3}(p,q)\eq{x_2\eq{A}x_2}p^{-1}\ct q\ct p.
            \]

            \item Conjugation by\/ $p$ is an equivalence\/ $(x_1\eq{A}x_1)\simeq(x_2\eq{A}x_2)$.
        \end{enumerate}
    \end{enumerate}
\end{lem}

\begin{proof}
    In the first case, both sides inhabit $a\eq Ax_2$. In the second, they inhabit $x_2\eq Aa$. In the third, $x_2\eq Ax_2$. Therefore, all types are verified.

    By path induction on $p$ we can assume that $x_2\equiv x_1$ and $p\equiv\fun{refl}_{x_1}$. In this case, each of the transport operations on the left-hand sides evaluates definitionally to $q$. On the right-hand side, the expressions reduce propositionally (with the second case reducing definitionally) to $q$ by the unit laws for path concatenation.

    Since the equations hold, the corresponding operations inherit the equivalence property of Theorem~\ref{thm:coherent-transport}.
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}
    Let\/ $A$ be a type, $B\colon A\to\mathcal{U}$ a type family, and consider two dependent functions\/ $f,g\colon\prod_{x\colon A}B(x)$. For any path\/ $p\colon a\eq Aa'$ and any path\/ $q\colon f(a)\eq{B(a)}g(a)$, transport in the identity family\/ $E(x)\defeq f(x)\eq{B(x)}g(x)$ computes as
    \[
        \fun{transport}^E(p,q)
            \eq{f(a')\eq{B(a')}g(a')} 
            \fun{apd}_f(p)^{-1}
                \ct\fun{ap}_{\fun{transport}^B(p)}(q)\ct\fun{apd}_g(p).
    \]
\end{thm}


\begin{proof}
    The type of the \lhs is clearly $f(a')\eq{B(a')}g(a')$. For the \rhs we have
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{apd}_f(p)^{-1}&\colon f(a')\eq{B(a')}p_*(f(a))\\
        \fun{transport}^B(p)&\colon B(a)\to B(a')\\
        \fun{ap}_{\fun{transport}^B(p)}(q)
            &\colon p_*(f(a))\eq{B(a')}p_*(g(a))\\
        \fun{apd}_g(p)&\colon p_*(g(a))\eq{B(a')}g(a').
    \end{align*}
    By path induction on $p$, we may assume that $a'\equiv a$ and $p\equiv\fun{refl}_a$. In this case, the transport operation on the \lhs evaluates definitionally to $q$. On the \rhs, the dependent action on paths $\fun{apd}$ for both $f$ and $g$ evaluates to $\fun{refl}_{f(a)}$ and $\fun{refl}_{g(a)}$, respectively. Furthermore, transport along $\fun{refl}_a$ in $B$ is definitionally the identity function, so its action on the path $q$ is simply $q$. The expression reduces to $\fun{refl}_{f(a)}^{-1}\ct q\ct\fun{refl}_{g(a)}$, which propositionally reduces to $q$ by the unit laws for path concatenation.
    \end{proof}

\section{Equality in Coproducts}\label{equality-in-coproducts}

To apply the observational framework \ref{observational-framework} to the coproduct $A+B$, we proceed as follows:
\begin{description}[font=\normalfont\scshape]
    \item[Code family.] By nesting the recursion principle $\fun{rec}_{A+B}$, we obtain
    \begin{align*}
        g_{00}\colon A&\to A\to\univ & g_{10}\colon B&\to A\to\univ\\
        a&\mapsto(a'\mapsto a\eq Aa') & b&\mapsto(a\mapsto\fun0)\\
        g_{01}\colon A&\to B\to\univ & g_{11}\colon B&\to B\to\univ\\
        a&\mapsto(b\mapsto\fun0) & b&\mapsto(b'\mapsto b\eq Bb')\\
        g_0\colon A&\to(A+B)\to\univ & g_1\colon B&\to(A+B)\to\univ\\
        a&\mapsto\fun{rec}_{A+B}(\univ,g_{00}(a),g_{01}(a)) & b&\mapsto\fun{rec}_{A+B}(\univ,g_{10}(b),g_{11}(b)).
    \end{align*}
    Then, we apply the recursion principle one final time to define the outer family:
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{Code}\colon(A+B)&\to(A+B)\to\univ\\
        z&\mapsto\fun{rec}_{A+B}((A+B)\to\univ,g_0,g_1,z),
    \end{align*}
    which can be represented by:
    \[
        \begin{tikzcd}[row sep=large, font=\small]
            & A \arrow[ld,"g_{00}"'] \arrow[d,"g_{01}"] \arrow[rrd,"g_0"] \\
            A\to\univ & B\to\univ & (A+B) \arrow[r,"\fun{Code}"] & (A+B)\to\univ \\
            & B \arrow[lu,"g_{10}"] \arrow[u,"g_{11}"'] \arrow[rru,"g_1"']
        \end{tikzcd}
    \]
    More commonly in practice, the $\fun{Code}$ function can be defined using pattern matching on the four constructors of $A+B$:
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{Code}\colon(A+B)\to(A+B)&\to\univ\\
        \fun{Code}(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inl}(y))&\defeq x\eq Ay\\
        \fun{Code}(\fun{inr}(x),\fun{inr}(y))&\defeq x\eq By\\
        \fun{Code}(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inr}(y))&\defeq\fun0\\
        \fun{Code}(\fun{inr}(x),\fun{inl}(y))&\defeq\fun0.
    \end{align*}

    \item[Base code.] Define the dependent function 
    \[
        c\colon\prod_{z\colon A+B}\fun{Code}(z,z)
    \]
    by induction on $z$. The motive is the type-valued function 
    \[
        C\defeq w\mapsto\fun{Code}(w,w).
    \]
    For the base cases, specify:
    \begin{align*}
        f_0&\colon\prod_{x\colon A}
            \fun{Code}(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inl}(x)) 
            &f_1&\colon\prod_{y\colon B}
            \fun{Code}(\fun{inr}(y),\fun{inr}(y))\\
        f_0(x)&\defeq\fun{refl}_x 
            &f_1(y)&\defeq\fun{refl}_y.
    \end{align*}
    Then, define
    \[
        c(z)\defeq\fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,f_0,f_1,z).
    \]
    In other words,
    \[
        c(z)\defeq\begin{cases}
            \fun{refl}_x    &\text{if }z\equiv\fun{inl}(x),\\
            \fun{refl}_y    &\text{if }z\equiv\fun{inr}(y).
        \end{cases}
    \]

    \item[Encode function.] We have to define
    \[
        \fun{encode}\colon\prod_{z,w\colon A+B}
            (z\eq{A+B}w)\to\fun{Code}(z,w).
    \]
    Fix $z\colon A+B$. Then, $C_z\defeq\fun{Code}(z,\dummy)$ and
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{encode}(z,w)\colon(z\eq{A+B}w)&\to\fun{Code}(z,w)\\
        p&\mapsto\fun{transport}^{C_z}(p,c(z)).
    \end{align*}
    In the case where $w\equiv z$ and $p\equiv\fun{refl}_z$, we have
    \[
        \fun{encode}(z,w)(\fun{refl}_z)\equiv c(z).
    \]
    
    \item[Decode function.] We have to define
    \[
        \fun{decode}\colon\prod_{z,w\colon A+B}
            \fun{Code}(z,w)\to(z\eq{A+B}w)
    \]

    
    By double induction on $z,w\colon A+B$, we define
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{decode}\colon&\prod_{z,w\colon A+B}\fun{Code}(z,w)
            \to(z\eq{A+B}w)\\
        &(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inl}(u))
            \mapsto\fun{ap}_{\fun{inl}}\\
        &(\fun{inr}(y),\fun{inr}(v))
            \mapsto\fun{ap}_{\fun{inr}}\\
        &(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inr}(v))\mapsto
            \fun{rec}_{\fun0}(\fun{inl}(x)\eq{A+B}\fun{inr}(v))\\
        &(\fun{inr}(y),\fun{inl}(u))\mapsto
            \fun{rec}_{\fun0}(\fun{inr}(y)\eq{A+B}\fun{inl}(u)).
    \end{align*}
    
    To verify that $\fun{decode}(z,w)$ is a right inverse of $\fun{encode}(z,w)$ for the left constructor, we must show that for any path $q\colon x\eq Au$,
    \[
        \fun{encode}(\fun{decode}(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inl}(u),q))
            \eq{x\eq Au}q.
    \]
    Since $\fun{ap}_{\fun{inl}}(q)\colon\fun{inl}(x)\eq{A+B}\fun{inl}(u)$, we have
    \begin{align*}
       \lhs&\equiv\fun{encode}(\fun{ap}_{\fun{inl}}(q))\\
            &\equiv
                \fun{transport}^{C_{\fun{inl}(x)}}
                    (\fun{ap}_{\fun{inl}}(q),\fun{refl}_x).
    \end{align*}
    Based path induction on $q$ reduces us to the case $u\equiv x$, $q\equiv\fun{refl}_x$. Here the \lhs becomes $\fun{refl}_x$, which is definitionally equal to $q$.
    
    The verification for the right constructor is similar.

    For the mixed case $z\equiv\fun{inl}(x)$ and $w\equiv\fun{inr}(v)$, the code $q$ inhabits the empty type, meaning $q\colon\fun0$. We must formally construct a term of type
    \[
        \fun{encode}(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inr}(v),\fun{decode}(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inr}(v),q)) = q.
    \]
    We proceed by induction on $q$ using the empty type eliminator $\fun{ind}_{\fun0}$. We define our motive $P\colon\fun0\to\univ$ as the identity family:
    \[
        P(q)\defeq
            \fun{encode}(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inr}
            (v),\fun{decode}(\fun{inl}(x),\fun{inr}(v),q))
            \eq{\fun0}
            q.
    \]
    Applying the eliminator to our motive $P$ and our element $q\colon\fun0$ directly yields the required proof term:
    \[
        \fun{ind}_{\fun0}(P,q)\colon
            \fun{encode}(\fun{inl}(x),
                \fun{inr}(v),
                \fun{decode}(\fun{inl}(x),
                \fun{inr}(v)
                ,q))
            \eq{\fun0}q.
    \]
    The symmetric mixed case is proven similarly.
\end{description}

For the Extended framework~\ref{extended-framework} we have to consider
\begin{description}[font=\normalfont\scshape]
    \item[Inversion map:] Defined by
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{inv}\colon\fun{Code}(z,w)&\to\fun{Code}(w,z)\\
        p&\mapsto p^{-1}
    \end{align*}

    \item[Concatenation:] Defined by
    \begin{align*}
        \ect\colon\fun{Code(x,y}\to\fun{Code}(y,z)
            &\to\fun{Code}(x,z)\\
        p\ect q&\defeq p\ct q.
    \end{align*}

    \item[Unit laws:] Since $c(z)$ is a reflection path, we have:
    \[
        \fun{inv}(c(z))\equiv c(z)
    \]
    and
    \[
        c(z)\ect q\equiv q.
    \]
\end{description}

\begin{lem}\label{lem:A+B-goal-reduction}
    The type
    \[
        \prod_{x,y\colon A+B}\;
        \prod_{\omega\colon\fun{Code}(x,y)}
        P(x,y,\omega)
    \]
    is inhabited if, and only if, the type
    \[
        \Bigl(
            \prod_{a\colon A}P(\fun{inl}(a),\fun{inl}(a),\fun{refl}_a)
        \Bigr)
        \times
        \Bigl(
            \prod_{b\colon B}P(\fun{inr}(b),\fun{inr}(b),\fun{refl}_b)
        \Bigr)
    \]
    has a witness.
\end{lem}

\begin{proof}
    By induction on\/ $x$ and\/ $y$, we may assume that both are constructed via the injections from\/ $A$ or\/ $B$. Since we are quantifying over an element\/ $\omega\colon\fun{Code}(x,y)$, we may disregard the mixed cases. Thus, we can assume that both injections come from\/ $A$ or both come from\/ $B$. The goal becomes
    \[
        \Bigl(\prod_{a,a'\colon A}\;
        \prod_{p\colon a\eq{A}a'}
        P(\fun{inl}(a),\fun{inl}(a'),p)\Bigr)
        \times
        \Bigl(\prod_{b,b'\colon B}\;\prod_{q\colon b\eq{B}b'}
        P(\fun{inr}(b),\fun{inr}(b'),q)\Bigr).
    \]
    The conclusion then follows by path induction on\/ $p$ and\/ $q$.
\end{proof}

\begin{prop}
    Let $A,B\colon\univ$ be types. Given $x,y,z\colon A+B$ we have
    \begin{enumerate}[a),font=\upshape]
        \item $\fun{decode}(x,x,c(x))\equiv c(z)$
        \item $\fun{decode}(y,x,q^{-1})\eq{y\eq{A+B}x}\fun{decode}(x,y,q)^{-1}$
        \item $\fun{decode}(x,z,q\ct r)\eq{x\eq{A+B}z}\fun{decode}(x,y,q)\ct\fun{decode}(y,z,r)$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{prop}

\begin{proof}
    This is a direct consequence of Theorem~\ref{thm:extended-framework}.\footnote{Parts b) and c) can also be easily deduced from Lemma~\ref{lem:A+B-goal-reduction}.}
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}
    Let\/ $X$ be a type and\/ $A,B\colon X\to\univ$ type families. Then, given a path\/ $p\colon x_1\eq{X}x_2$, we have
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{transport}^{A+B}(p,\fun{inl}(a))
            &\eq{A(x_2)+B(x_2)}\fun{inl}(\fun{transport}^A(p,a)),\\
        \fun{transport}^{A+B}(p,\fun{inr}(b))
            &\eq{A(x_2)+B(x_2)}\fun{inr}(\fun{transport}^B(p,b)),
    \end{align*}
    where $(A+B)(x)\defeq A(x)+B(x)$ for $x\colon X$.
    
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    Clearly both sides of both equalities are of type $A(x_2)+B(x_2)$. Therefore, we can proceed by induction on $p$. This reduces ourselves to the case $x_2\equiv x_1$ and $p\equiv\fun{refl}_{x_1}$. Then all transport become identities and the equalities are trivially inhabited, the first by $\fun{refl}_{\fun{inl}(a)}$ and the second by $\fun{refl}_{\fun{inr}(b)}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}\label{thm:pair-f+g}
    Let\/ $f\colon A\to A'$ and\/ $g\colon B\to B'$ be two functions.
    \begin{enumerate}[a),font=\upshape]
        \item For all\/ $x_0,y_0\colon A$ and paths\/ $p\colon\fun{inl}(x_0)\eq{A+B}\fun{inl}(y_0)$, we have
        \[
            \fun{ap}_{f+g}(p)
            \eq{\fun{inl}(f(x_0))\eq{A'+B'}\fun{inl}(f(y_0))}
            \fun{decode}(\fun{ap}_f(\fun{encode}(p))).
        \]
        
        \item For all\/ $x_1,y_1\colon B$ and paths\/ $q\colon\fun{inr}(x_1)\eq{A+B}\fun{inr}(y_1)$, we have
        \[
            \fun{ap}_{f+g}(q)
            \eq{\fun{inr}(g(x_1))\eq{A'+B'}\fun{inr}(g(y_1))}
            \fun{decode}(\fun{ap}_g(\fun{encode}(q))).
        \]
        
        \item For all\/ $x_0\colon A$,\/ $y_1\colon B$, and paths\/ $r\colon\fun{inl}(x_0)\eq{A+B}\fun{inr}(y_1)$, we have
        \[
            \fun{ap}_{f+g}(r)
            \eq{\fun{inl}(f(x_0))\eq{A'+B'}\fun{inr}(g(y_1))}
            \fun{ind}_{\fun0}(\fun{encode}(r)).
        \]
        
        \item For all\/ $x_1\colon B$,\/ $y_0\colon A$, and paths\/ $s\colon\fun{inr}(x_1)\eq{A+B}\fun{inl}(y_0)$, we have
        \[
            \fun{ap}_{f+g}(s)
            \eq{\fun{inr}(g(x_1))\eq{A'+B'}\fun{inl}(f(y_0))}
            \fun{ind}_{\fun0}(\fun{encode}(s)).
        \]
    \end{enumerate}
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}${}$
    \begin{enumerate}[a),font=\upshape]
        \item By path induction on $p$, we may assume $y_0\equiv x_0$ and $p\equiv\fun{refl}_{\fun{inl}(x_0)}$. Then,
        \begin{align*}
            \lhs&\equiv
                \fun{refl}_{f+g(\fun{refl}_{\fun{inl}(x_0)})}\\
                &\equiv\fun{refl}_{\fun{inl}(f(x_0))}.\\[1mm]
            \fun{encode}(p)&\equiv\fun{refl}_{x_0}.\\
            \fun{ap}_f(\fun{encode}(p))&\equiv
                \fun{refl}_{f(x_0)}.\\
            \rhs&\equiv\fun{refl}_{\fun{inl}(f(x_0))}.
        \end{align*}

        \item Similarly, by path induction on $q$
        .
        \item Since $r\colon\fun{inl}(x_0)\eq{A+B}\fun{inr}(y_1)$ is a path between distinct constructors, $\fun{encode}(r)$ yields an element of the empty type $\fun{0}$. The equality is therefore vacuously inhabited via $\fun{ind}_{\fun0}$.
        
        \item Similar to part c).
    \end{enumerate}
\end{proof}

\section{Equality in Natural Numbers}\label{equality-in-N}

In this section we apply the Observational Framework \ref{observational-framework} to the type of natural numbers.
\begin{description}[font=\normalfont\scshape]
    \item[Code family.] We define the family $\fun{Code}\colon\N\to\N\to\univ$ formally using nested recursion. 
    
    For the base case, we define
    \[
        e_0\colon\N\to\univ;\quad
        e_0\defeq\fun{rec}_\N(\univ,e_{00},e_{0s}),
    \]
    where
    \begin{align*}
        e_{00}&\colon\univ
        &e_{0s}\colon\N\to\univ&\to\univ\\
        e_{00}&\defeq\fun1
        &(m,X)&\mapsto\fun0.
    \end{align*}
    By definition, $e_0$ satisfies
    \[
        e_0(0)\equiv\fun1
        \quad\text{and}\quad
        e_0(\fun{succ}(m))\equiv\fun0.
    \]
    For the inductive step, we define the function
    \[
        e_s\colon\N\to(\N\to\univ)\to(\N\to\univ).
    \]
    Given $n\colon\N$ and a function $e\colon\N\to\univ$ (representing the recursive evaluation at $n$), we define the next function $e_s(n,e)$ by recursion on its argument:
    \[
        e_s(n,e)\defeq\fun{rec}_\N(\univ,e_{s0},e_{ss}),
    \]
    where
    \begin{align*}
        e_{s0}&\colon\univ
        &e_{ss}\colon\N\to\univ&\to\univ\\
        e_{s0}&\defeq\fun0
        &(m,Y)&\mapsto e(m).
    \end{align*}
    By definition, we have $e_s(n,e)(0)\equiv\fun0$ and
    \begin{align*}
        e_s(n,e)(\fun{succ}(m))
            &\equiv\fun{rec}_\N(\univ,e_{s0},e_{ss},\fun{succ}(m))\\
            &\equiv e_{ss}(m,\fun{rec}_\N(\univ,e_{s0},e_{ss},m))\\
            &\equiv e_{ss}(m,\underbrace{e_s(n,e)(m)}_Y)\\[-4.5mm]
            &\equiv e(m).
    \end{align*}
    Finally, we define $\fun{Code}$ by applying the outer recursor with motive $\N\to\univ$:
    \[
        \fun{Code}\defeq\fun{rec}_\N(\N\to\univ,e_0,e_s).
    \]
    In particular, we have
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{Code}(0,0)
            &\equiv e_0(0)\equiv\fun1\\
        \fun{Code}(0,\fun{succ}(m))
            &\equiv e_0(\fun{succ}(m))\equiv\fun0\\
        \fun{Code}(\fun{succ}(n),0)
            &\equiv e_s(n,\fun{Code}(n))(0)\\
            &\equiv e_{s0}\\
            &\equiv\fun0\\
        \fun{Code}(\fun{succ}(n),\fun{succ}(m))
            &\equiv e_s(n,\fun{Code}(n))(\fun{succ}(m))\\
            &\equiv\fun{Code}(n)(m)\\
            &\equiv\fun{Code}(n,m).
    \end{align*}

    \item[Base code.] To define the base code we need the following
    
    \begin{lem}
        Given $n\colon\N$, the type $\fun{Code}(n,n)\eq\univ\fun1$ is inhabited.
    \end{lem}
    
    \begin{proof}
        We proceed by induction on $n$, with the motive $C\colon\N\to\univ$ specified as
        \[
            C(n)\defeq\fun{Code}(n,n)\eq\univ\fun1.
        \]
        For the base case we can take $\fun{refl}_{\fun1}$.
        
        Given a witness of $C(n)$, i.e., a path $h\colon\fun{Code}(n,n)\eq\univ\fun1$, we define the step function
        \begin{align*}
            e_s&\colon\prod_{n\colon\N}C(n)\to C(\fun{succ}(n))\\
            e_s(n,h)&\defeq h,
        \end{align*}
        which is well defined as the following diagram of paths demonstrates
        \[
            \begin{tikzcd}
                {\fun{Code}(n,n)}
                        \arrow[d,"h"']
                        \arrow[r,"\equiv"]
                    &{\fun{Code}(\fun{succ}(n),\fun{succ}(n))}
                        \arrow[ld,"h"]\\
                \fun1
            \end{tikzcd}
        \]
        Consequently, we have
        \[
            e(n)\defeq\fun{ind}_\N(C,\fun{refl}_{\fun1},e_s,n)
                \colon\fun{Code}(n,n)\eq\univ\fun1.
        \]
    \end{proof}
    Note that, given $n\colon\N$, to define the base code $c(n)\colon\fun{Code}(n,n)$, we cannot simply select $\fun\star\colon\fun1$ because that would not type-check. Instead, we need to transport $\fun\star$ as follows:
    \[
        c(n)\defeq\fun{transport}^{\id_\univ}(e(n)^{-1},\fun\star),
    \]
    where $e(n)\colon\fun{Code}(n,n)\eq\univ\fun1$ is the witness given by the lemma. In particular,
    \[
        e(0)\equiv\fun{refl}_{\fun1}
        \quad\text{and}\quad
        c(0)\equiv\fun\star.
    \]
    More generally,
    \begin{align*}
        e(\fun{succ}(n))
            &\equiv\fun{ind}_\N(C,\fun{refl}_{\fun1},e_s,\fun{succ}(n))\\
            &\equiv e_s(n,e(n))\\
            &\equiv e(n),
    \end{align*}
    which implies that $c(\fun{succ}(n))\equiv c(n)$.

    \item[Encode function.] Define
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{encode}&\colon\prod_{n,m\colon\N}
            (n\eq{\N}m)\to\fun{Code}(n,m)\\
        \fun{encode}(n,m,p)&\defeq\fun{transport}^{C_n}(p,c(n)).
    \end{align*}
    In particular, given $p\colon0\eq\N0$, since $C_0(0)\equiv\fun{Code}(0,0)\equiv\fun1$, we have
    \[
        \fun{transport}^{C_0}(p)\colon\fun1\to\fun1
    \]
    and so
    \begin{align}\label{eq:case-0,0}
        \fun{encode}(0,0,p)
            &\equiv\fun{transport}^{C_0}(p,\fun\star)
                \nonumber\\
            &\eq{\fun1}\fun\star,
    \end{align}
    where the last propositional equality holds because every inhabitant of the unit type $\fun1$ is propositionally equal to $\fun\star$ via $\fun{uniq}_{\fun1}$.
    

    \item[Decode function.] To define
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{decode}&\colon\prod_{n,m\colon\N}
            \fun{Code}(n,m)\to(n\eq\N m)
    \end{align*}
    we proceed by nested induction.

    For the base case, we take the motive
    \begin{align*}
         D_0&\colon\N\to\univ\\
         D_0(m)&\defeq\fun{Code}(0,m)\to(0\eq\N m),
    \end{align*}
    and define
    \[
        d_0\colon\prod_{m\colon\N}D_0(m);
            \quad d_0\defeq\fun{ind}_\N(D_0,d_{00},d_{0s}),
    \]
    where
    \begin{align*}
        d_{00}\colon\fun{Code}(0,0)&\to(0\eq\N0)\\
        \fun\star&\mapsto\fun{refl}_0\\
        d_{0s}\colon\prod_{m\colon\N}D_0(m)&\to D_0(\fun{succ}(m))\\
        (m,d)&\mapsto\fun{rec}_{\fun0}(0\eq\N\fun{succ}(m)).
    \end{align*}
    In particular,
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:d0}
        \begin{aligned}
            d_0(0)
                &\equiv\fun\star\mapsto\fun{refl}_0\\
            d_0(\fun{succ}(m))
                &\equiv d_{0s}(m,d_0(m))\\
                &\equiv\fun{rec}_{\fun0}(0\eq\N\fun{succ}(m)).
        \end{aligned}
    \end{equation}
    Now we introduce the motive, which fixes the variable $n$, by
    \begin{align*}
        D&\colon\N\to\univ\\
        D(n)&\defeq\prod_{m\colon\N}\fun{Code}(n,m)\to(n\eq\N m),
    \end{align*}
    and define
    \begin{align*}
        d_s&\colon\prod_{n\colon\N}D(n)\to D(\fun{succ}(n))\\
        d_s(n,h)&\defeq\fun{ind}_\N(D_{nh},d_{s0},d_{ss})
    \end{align*}
    where $h\colon D(n)$, $D_{nh}(m)\defeq\fun{Code}(\fun{succ}(n),m)\to(\fun{succ}(n)\eq\N m)$ and
    \begin{align*}
        d_{s0}&\colon D_{nh}(0)\\
        d_{s0}&\defeq\fun{rec}_{\fun0}(\fun{succ}(n)\eq\N0)\\
        d_{ss}&\colon\prod_{m\colon\N}D_{nh}(m)\to D_{nh}(\fun{succ}(m))\\
        d_{ss}(m,\omega,t)&\defeq\fun{ap}_{\fun{succ}}(h(m,t)),
    \end{align*}
    as illustrated in the following diagram
    \[
        \begin{tikzcd}[column sep=huge,row sep=large]
            {\fun{Code}(\fun{succ}(n),\fun{succ}(m))}
                    \arrow[r,"{d_{ss}(m,\omega)}"]
                &\fun{succ}(n)\eq\N\fun{succ}(m)\\
            {\fun{Code}(n,m)}
                    \arrow[r,"h(m)"']
                    \arrow[u,"\equiv",no head]
                &n\eq\N m
                    \arrow[u,"\fun{ap}_{\fun{succ}}"']
        \end{tikzcd}
    \]
    In particular,
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:ds}
        \begin{aligned}
            d_s(n,h,0)
                &\equiv\fun{rec}_{\fun0}(\fun{succ}(n)\eq\N0)\\
            d_s(n,h,\fun{succ}(m),t)
                &\equiv d_{ss}(m,d_s(n,h,m),t)\\
                &\equiv\fun{ap}_{\fun{succ}}(h(m,t))
        \end{aligned}
    \end{equation}
    This allows us to define
    \[
        \fun{decode}\defeq\fun{ind}_\N(D,d_0,d_s).
    \]
    From equations \eqref{eq:d0} and \eqref{eq:ds}, it follows
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:decode}
        \begin{aligned}
            \fun{decode}(0,0,\fun\star)
                &\equiv d_0(0,\fun\star)\\
                &\equiv\fun{refl}_0.\\[1mm]
            \fun{decode}(0,\fun{succ}(m))
                &\equiv d_0(\fun{succ}(m))\\
                &\equiv\fun{rec}_{\fun0}(0\eq\N\fun{succ}(m)).\\[1mm]
            \fun{decode}(\fun{succ}(n),0)
                &\equiv d_s(n,\fun{decode}(n),0)\\
                &\equiv\fun{rec}_{\fun0}(\fun{succ}(n)\eq\N0).\\[1mm]
            \fun{decode}(\fun{succ}(n),\fun{succ}(m),t)
                &\equiv d_s(n,\fun{decode}(n),\fun{succ}(m),t)\\
                &\equiv\fun{ap}_{\fun{succ}}(\fun{decode}(n,m,t)).
        \end{aligned}
    \end{equation}
\end{description}

\begin{thm}\label{thm:N-equality-encode-decode}
    For\/ $n,m\colon\N$, we have
    \[
        \fun{encode}(n,m)\circ\fun{decode}(n,m)
            \sim\id_{\fun{Code}(n,m)}.
    \]
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    We proceed by double induction, examining the distinct cases for the generic pair\/ $(n,m)$.
    \begin{description}
        \item[Case $(0,0)$:]
        \begin{align*}
            \fun{encode}(\fun{decode}(0,0,\fun\star))
                &\equiv\fun{encode}(\fun{refl}_0)\\
                &\equiv\fun{transport}^{C_0}(\fun{refl}_0,c(0))\\
                &\equiv c(0)\\
                &\equiv\fun\star.
        \end{align*}
        \item[Case $(0,\fun{succ}(m))$:] By \eqref{eq:decode} we know that $\dom(\fun{decode}(0,\fun{succ}(m)))\equiv\fun0$. Thus, for $c\colon\fun0$, we have
        \begin{align*}
            \fun{encode}(\fun{decode}(0,\fun{succ}(m),c))&\eq{\fun0}c,
        \end{align*}
        as it follows from $\fun{ind}_{\fun0}(E)\colon\prod_{x\colon\fun0}x\eq{\fun0}c$, where $E\colon\fun0\to\univ$ is given by $E(x)\defeq x\eq{\fun0}c$.

        \item[Case $(0,m)$:] It follows by induction on $m$ from the two previous cases.

        \item[Case $(\fun{succ}(n),0)$:] This is similar to the previous case because, by \eqref{eq:decode}, we know that $\dom(\fun{decode}(\fun{succ}(n),0))\equiv\fun0$.

         \item[Case $(n,0)$:] It follows from the cases $(0,0)$ and $(\fun{succ}(n),0)$ by induction on~$n$.

         \item[Case $(\fun{succ}(n),\fun{succ}(m))$:] By \eqref{eq:decode} and the definition of $\fun{encode}$, we have
         \begin{align*}
            \fun{encode}&(\fun{decode}(\fun{succ}(n),\fun{succ}(m),t))\\
                &\equiv\fun{transport}^{C_{\fun{succ}(n)}}
                    (\fun{ap}_{\fun{succ}}(\fun{decode}(n,m,t)),c(\fun{succ}(n)))\\
                &\eq{\fun{Code}(n,m)}\fun{transport}^{C_{\fun{succ}(n)}\circ\fun{succ}}
                    (\fun{decode}(n,m,t),c(n))\\
                &\equiv\fun{transport}^{C_n}
                    (\fun{decode}(n,m,t),c(n))\\
                &\equiv\fun{encode}(\fun{decode}(n,m,t)),
         \end{align*}
         where we have used Lemma~\ref{lem:post-composition-transport} and the identities
         \[
            c(\fun{succ}(n))\equiv c(n)
            \quad\text{and}\quad
            C_{\fun{succ}(n)}\circ\fun{succ}\equiv C_n.
        \]
        \item[Case $(n,m)$:] We assemble the preceding cases into a single term using the induction principle for the natural numbers. The main motive is
        \[
            E(n)\defeq\prod_{m\colon\N}
                \;\prod_{t\colon\fun{Code}(n,m)}\fun{encode}
                    (\fun{decode}(n,m,t))\eq{\fun{Code}(n,m)}t.
        \]
        A section of this type family is $\fun{ind}_\N(E,d_0,d_s)$, where:
        \begin{enumerate}[-]
            \item The base case $d_0\colon E(0)$ is constructed by induction on $m$. Concretely, $d_0\defeq\fun{ind}_\N(E_0,d_{00},d_{0s})$, with motive $E_0\defeq E(0)$, i.e.,
            \[
                E_0(m)\defeq\prod_{t\colon\fun{Code}(0,m)}\fun{encode}(\fun{decode}(0,m,t))\eq{\fun{Code}(0,m)}t.
            \]
            The base term $d_{00}$ is the proof from Case $(0,0)$, and the step term $d_{0s}$ is the proof from Case $(0,\fun{succ}(m))$.
            
            \item The step function $d_s\colon\prod_{n\colon\N} E(n)\to E(\fun{succ}(n))$ binds an index $n$ and the inductive hypothesis $h\colon E(n)$. Then $d_s(n,h)$ is defined by another induction on $m$, using the motive $E_s\defeq E(\fun{succ}(n))$, i.e.,
            \[
                E_s(m)\defeq
                    \prod_t
                    \fun{encode}(\fun{decode}(\fun{succ}(n),m,t))\eq{\fun{Code}(\fun{succ}(n),m)}t,
            \]
            for $t\colon\fun{Code}(\fun{succ}(n),m)$.
            
            The base case is provided directly by Case $(\fun{succ}(n),0)$. The step function is given in Case $(\fun{succ}(n),\fun{succ}(m))$ that reduces the goal to proving that
            \[
                \fun{encode}(\fun{decode}(n,m,t))\eq{\fun{Code}(n,m)}t,
            \]
            which is inhabited by $h(m,t)$.
        \end{enumerate}
        Thus, the total application of $\fun{ind}_\N$ yields the required dependent function for all $n$ and $m$.

    \end{description}
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}\label{thm:N-equality-decode-encode}
    For all\/ $n,m\colon\N$, we have
    \[
        \fun{decode}(n,m)\circ\fun{encode}(n,m)\sim\id_{n\eq\N m}.
    \]  
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    Consider the motive
    \[
        \prod_{n,m\colon\N}\;
        \prod_{p\colon n\eq\N m}
            \fun{decode}(n,m,\fun{encode}(n,m,p))\eq{n\eq\N m}p.
    \]
    To show that it is inhabited we can use path induction and reduce it to the case $m\equiv n$ and $p\equiv\fun{refl}_n$. The goal becomes
    \[
        D\defeq\prod_{n\colon\N}
            \fun{decode}
                (n,
                n,
                \fun{encode}(n,n,\fun{refl}_n))
            \eq{n\eq\N n}\fun{refl}_n.
    \]
    We can now proceed by induction on $n$.
    \begin{description}
        \item[Case $0$:]
        \begin{align*}
            \fun{decode}(0,0,\fun{encode}(0,0,\fun{refl}_0))
                &\equiv\fun{decode}(0,0,\fun\star)
                    &&;\ \eqref{eq:case-0,0}\\
                &\equiv\fun{refl}_0.
                    &&;\ \eqref{eq:decode}
        \end{align*}
        which is inhabited by $\fun{refl}_{\fun{refl}_0}\colon D(0)$.
        
        \item[Case $\fun{succ}(n)$:]
        First observe that
        \begin{align*}
            \fun{encode}
                    (\fun{succ}(n),
                    \fun{succ}(n),
                    \fun{refl}_{\fun{succ}(n)})
                &\equiv\fun{transport}^{C_{\fun{succ}(n)}}
                    (\fun{refl}_{\fun{succ}(n)},c(\fun{succ}(n)))\\
                &\equiv c(\fun{succ}(n))\\
                &\equiv c(n)\\
                &\equiv\fun{transport}^{C_n}(\fun{refl}_n,c(n))\\
                &\equiv\fun{encode}(n,n,\fun{refl}_n).
        \end{align*}
        Therefore,
        \begin{align*}
            D(\fun{succ}(n))
                &\equiv\fun{decode}
                    (\fun{succ}(n),
                    \fun{succ}(n),
                    \fun{encode}(n,n,\fun{refl}_n))\\
                &\equiv\fun{ap}_{\fun{succ}}
                    (\fun{decode}(n,n,\fun{encode}(n,n,\fun{refl}_n)))
                    &&;\ \eqref{eq:decode}
        \end{align*}
        Hence, if $h\colon D(n)$, then $\fun{ap}_{\fun{ap}_{\fun{succ}}}(h)\colon D(\fun{succ}(n))$, which defines the step function $D(n)\to D(\fun{succ}(n)$.
    \end{description}
\end{proof}

\section{Equality in Semigroups}

Let $A\colon\univ$ be a type. Using the type family
\begin{align*}
    M\colon\univ&\to\univ\\
    X&\mapsto(X\to X\to X)
\end{align*}
we define an \textsl{operation} on $A$ as a function
\[
    m\colon M(A).
\]
The operation $m$ is \textsl{associative} if the type
\[
    \fun{Assoc}(A,m)\defeq\prod_{x,y,z\colon A}
        m(x,m(y,z))\eq{A} m(m(x,y),z)
\]
is inhabited.

With this we can define a \textsl{semigroup structure} type on $A$ as
\begin{align*}
    \fun{SemigroupStr}&\colon\univ\to\univ\\
    \fun{SemigroupStr}(A)&\defeq\sum_{m\colon M(A)}
        \fun{Assoc}(A,m).
\end{align*}
More generally, the \textsl{Semigroup} type is
\[
    \fun{Semigroup}\defeq\sum_{A\colon\univ}\fun{SemigroupStr}(A).
\]
By viewing associativity as the dependent function
\[
    \fun{Assoc}\colon\prod_{X\colon\univ}M(X)\to\univ,
\]
we can uncurry it to obtain the type family
\begin{align*}
    \fun{Assoc}_{\Sigma}\colon\Sigma_\univ M&\to\univ\\
    \dep{A,m}&\mapsto\fun{Assoc}(A,m),
\end{align*}
where $\Sigma_\univ M$ is the total space $\sum_{X\colon\univ}M(X)$. With this notation we have
\[
    \fun{SemigroupStr}(A)
        \equiv\sum_{m\colon M(A)}\fun{Assoc}_\Sigma(A,m)
        \equiv\Sigma_M\fun{Assoc}_\Sigma(A)
\]
and
\[
    \fun{Semigroup}\equiv\Sigma_\univ(\Sigma_M\fun{Assoc}_\Sigma).
\]
Now suppose we are given a path $p\colon A\eq{\univ} B$ between the types $A$ and $B$. Then we can transport the operation $m$ on $A$ to an operation $m'$ on $B$ by
\[
    m'\defeq\fun{transport}^M(p,m).
\]
By path induction on $p$ it is easy to see that $m'$ is associative whenever $m$ is. However, we can go deeper if we apply Theorem~\ref{thm:composite-transport} as follows:
\[
    \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.6}
    \begin{tabular}{lll}
        \hline
        \textbf{Role}
        &\textbf{Theorem}
        &\textbf{Semigroup}\\
        \hline
        Base space
        &$X$
            &$\univ$\\
        First family
        &$A\colon X\to\univ$
            &$M\colon\univ\to\univ$\\
        Total space
        &$\Sigma_XA$
            &$\Sigma_\univ M$\\
        Second family
        &$B\colon\Sigma_XA\to\univ$ 
            &$\fun{Assoc}_{\Sigma}$\\
        Composite family
        &$\Sigma_AB\colon X\to\univ$
            &$\fun{SemigroupStr}$\\
        Equivalent family
        &$\Sigma_X(\Sigma_AB)$
            &$\fun{Semigroup}$\\
        \hline
    \end{tabular}
\]
with diagram correspondence:
\[
    \begin{tikzcd}[row sep=large,font=\small]
        \Sigma_X(\Sigma_AB)
                \arrow[rdd,"\pi_1^{\Sigma_AB}"']
            &\Sigma_{\Sigma_XA}B
                \arrow[d,"\pi_1^B"]
                \arrow[l,"\simeq"']\\
            &\Sigma_XA
                \arrow[d,"\pi_1^A"]
                \arrow[r,"B"]
            &\univ\\
        \univ
            &X
                \arrow[l,"\Sigma_AB"]
                \arrow[r,"A"']
            &\univ
    \end{tikzcd}
    \quad
    \begin{tikzcd}[row sep=large,font=\small]
        \fun{Semigroup}
                \arrow[rdd,"\pi_1^{\fun{SemigroupStr}}"']
            &\Sigma_{\Sigma_\univ M}\fun{Assoc}_\Sigma
                \arrow[d,"\pi_1^{\fun{Assoc}_\Sigma}"]
                \arrow[l,"\simeq"']\\
            &\Sigma_\univ M
                \arrow[d,"\pi_1^M"]
                \arrow[r,"\fun{Assoc}_\Sigma"]
            &\univ\\
        \univ
            &\univ
                \arrow[l,"\fun{SemigroupStr}"]
                \arrow[r,"M"']
            &\univ
    \end{tikzcd}
\]
Given a path $p\colon A\eq{\univ} B$ between types, part b) of the theorem establishes the equality
\[
    \fun{transport}^{\Sigma_AB}(p)(\dep{u,z})
        \eq{\Sigma_AB(B)}
        \bigl(
            p_*(u),\fun{transport}^B
                (\fun{pair}^=(\dep{p,\fun{refl}_{p_*(u)}}))(z)
        \bigr),
\]
which, according to our table above, translates into      
\[
    \fun{transport}^{\fun{SemigroupStr}}(p)(\dep{m,a})
    \eq{\fun{SemigroupStr}(B)}
    (\dep{m',a'})
\]
where
\begin{align*}
    m'&\defeq\fun{transport}^M(p,m)\\
    a'&\defeq\fun{transport}^{\fun{Assoc}_{\Sigma}}
        \bigl(\fun{pair}^=(p,\fun{refl}_{m'}),a\bigr).
\end{align*}
Note that
\[
    \fun{pair}^=(p,\fun{refl}_{m'})\colon(\dep{A,m})
        \eq{\Sigma_\univ M}(\dep{B,m'})
\]
serves as the base path to transport the associativity proof $a$ into its new fiber $\fun{Assoc}_{\Sigma}(B,m')$.

We have proven the following
\begin{thm}\ua
    Let\/ $A$ and\/ $B$ be types, and let\/ $\dep{m,a}$ be a semigroup structure on\/ $A$. Given an equivalence\/ $e\colon A\simeq B$, the univalence axiom induces a semigroup structure\/ $(m',a')$ on\/ $B$ defined by
    \begin{align*}
        m'&\defeq\fun{transport}^M(\fun{ua}(e),m)\\
        a'&\defeq\fun{transport}^{\fun{Assoc}_{\Sigma}}
            \bigl(\fun{pair}^=(\dep{\fun{ua}(e),\fun{refl}_{m'}}),a\bigr).
    \end{align*}
\end{thm}

\section{Universal Properties}

In category theory, universal properties are typically expressed by the unique existence of morphisms that complete commutative diagrams. In homotopy type theory, however, these properties are interpreted homotopically as equivalences between types. 

These universal properties can generally be categorized by whether they characterize functions \emph{into} a type (reflecting its \textsl{introduction} rules) or functions \emph{out of} a type (reflecting its \textsl{elimination} or \textsl{induction} principles).

As we discussed in \nref{lpar:products-revisited}, the cartesian product $A\times B$ admits two characterizations: one by means of a function into $A\times B$, and another by means of a function out of $A\times B$. In each of these cases, the associated commutative diagram can be represented as an equivalence of types. In this section, we generalize this approach to different basic types.

\begin{thm}\fe
    Let\/ $X,A,B\colon\univ$ be types. Then the function\footnote{By an abuse of notation, we omit irrelevant parentheses.}
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma\colon(X\to A\times B)&\to(X\to A)\times(X\to B)\\
        f&\mapsto(\pi_1\circ f,\pi_2\circ f)
    \end{align*}
    is an equivalence.
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    Define the map
    \begin{align*}
        \tau\colon(X\to A)\times(X\to B)&\to(X\to A\times B)\\
        \tau(u,v)&\defeq\lambda x.\,\bigl(u(x),v(x)\bigr).
    \end{align*}
    Evaluating $\sigma$ at $\tau(u,v)$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma(\tau(u,v))
            &\equiv\sigma\bigl(\lambda x.\,(u(x),v(x))\bigr)\\
            &\equiv\bigl(\pi_1\circ(\lambda x.\,(u(x),v(x))),\pi_2\circ(\lambda x.\,(u(x),v(x)))\bigr)\\
            &\equiv\bigl(\lambda x.\,\pi_1(u(x),v(x)),\lambda x.\,\pi_2(u(x),v(x))\bigr)\\
            &\equiv\bigl(\lambda x.\,u(x),\lambda x.\,v(x)\bigr)\\
            &\equiv(u,v).
    \end{align*}
    On the other hand, evaluating $\tau$ at $\sigma(f)$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \tau(\sigma(f))
            &\equiv\tau(\pi_1\circ f,\pi_2\circ f)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,\bigl(\pi_1(f(x)),\pi_2(f(x))\bigr).
    \end{align*}
    By Corollary~\ref{cor:cartesian-uniqueness}, there is a path in $(\pi_1(f(x)),\pi_2(f(x)))\eq{A\times B}f(x)$ for each $x\colon X$. Therefore, applying the function extensionality axiom to this pointwise equality yields the path
    \[
        \tau(\sigma(f))\eq{X\to A\times B}f,
    \]
    which completes the proof by showing that $\tau$ is a quasi-inverse of~$\sigma$.
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}\fe
    Let\/ $X\colon\univ$ be a type, and let\/ $A,B\colon X\to\univ$ be type families. Then the function
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma\colon
                \biggl(
                    \prod_{x\colon X}A(x)\times B(x)
                \biggr)
            &\to
                \biggl(
                    \prod_{x\colon X}
                        A(x)\biggr)\times\biggl(\prod_{x\colon X}B(x)
                \biggr)\\
        f&\mapsto
            \bigl(
                \lambda x.\,\pi_1(f(x)),\lambda x.\,\pi_2(f(x))
            \bigr)
    \end{align*}
    is an equivalence.
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    Define the map
    \begin{align*}
        \tau\colon
            \biggl(
                    \prod_{x\colon X}
                        A(x)\biggr)\times\biggl(\prod_{x\colon X}B(x)
                \biggr)
            &\to\prod_{x\colon X}A(x)\times B(x)\\
            (u,v)&\mapsto
                \lambda x.\,(u(x),v(x)).
    \end{align*}
    Evaluating $\sigma\circ\tau$ at $(u,v)$, we obtain
    \begin{align*}
        \sigma(\tau(u,v))
            &\equiv\sigma\bigl(\lambda x.\,(u(x),v(x))\bigr)\\
            &\equiv
                \bigl(
                    \lambda x.\,\pi_1(u(x),v(x)),
                    \lambda x.\,\pi_2(u(x),v(x))
                \bigr)\\
            &\equiv\bigl(\lambda x.\,u(x),\lambda x.\,v(x)\bigr)\\
            &\equiv(u,v).
    \end{align*}
    Evaluating $\tau\circ\sigma$ at $f$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \tau(\sigma(f))
            &\equiv\tau
                \bigl(
                    \lambda x.\,\pi_1(f(x)),\lambda x.\,\pi_2(f(x))
                \bigr)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,
                \bigl(\pi_1(f(x)),\pi_2(f(x))\bigr).
    \end{align*}
    By Corollary~\ref{cor:cartesian-uniqueness}, for each $x\colon X$, the type $(\pi_1(f(x)),\pi_2(f(x)))\eq{A(x)\times B(x)}f(x)$ is inhabited. Thus, function extensionality implies that
    \[
        \tau(\sigma(f))\eq\Gamma f,
    \]
    where
    \[
        \Gamma\defeq\prod_{x\colon X}A(x)\times B(x).
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}\fe{\upshape[Axiom of Choice]}\label{thm:ac-generalized}
    Let\/ $X\colon\univ$ be a type, $A\colon X\to\univ$ a type family, and\/ $P\colon\prod_{x\colon X}A(x)\to\univ$ a dependent type family. Then the function
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{ac}\colon
            \biggl(
                \prod_{x\colon X}\;\sum_{y\colon A(x)}P(x,y)
            \biggr)
            &\to
                \sum_{g\colon\prod_{x\colon X}A(x)}\;
                    \prod_{x\colon X}P(x,g(x))\\
        f&\mapsto
            \bigdep{
                \lambda x.\,\pi_1(f(x)),\lambda x.\,\pi_2(f(x))
            }
    \end{align*}
    is an equivalence.
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    First observe that $\fun{ac}$ is well defined:
    \begin{align*}
        f(x)&\colon\sum_{y\colon A(x)}P(x,y)\\
    \intertext{and so}
        \pi_1(f(x))&\colon A(x)\\
        \pi_2(f(x))&\colon P(x,\pi_1(f(x)).
    \end{align*}
    
    Consider the function
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{ac}^{-1}\colon
            \Biggl(
                \sum_{g\colon\prod_{x\colon X}A(x)}\;
                    \prod_{x\colon X}P(x,g(x))
            \Biggr)
        &\to
            \prod_{x\colon X}\;\sum_{y\colon A(x)}P(x,y)\\
        \dep{g,\varepsilon}
            &\mapsto\lambda x.\,\dep{g(x),\varepsilon(x)}.
    \end{align*}
    Then,
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{ac}(\fun{ac}^{-1}(\dep{g,\varepsilon}))
            &\equiv\fun{ac}
                \bigl(
                    \lambda x.\,\dep{g(x),\varepsilon(x)}
                \bigr)\\
            &\equiv
                \bigdep{
                    \lambda x.\,\pi_1(g(x),\varepsilon(x)),
                    \lambda x.\,\pi_2(g(x),\varepsilon(x))
                }\\
            &\equiv
                \bigdep{
                    \lambda x.\,g(x),\lambda x.\,\varepsilon(x)
                }\\
            &\equiv\dep{g,\varepsilon}.
    \end{align*}
    On the other hand,
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{ac}^{-1}(\fun{ac}(f))
            &\equiv\fun{ac}^{-1}
                \bigl(
                    \dep{
                        \overbrace{\lambda x.\,\pi_1(f(x))}^g,
                        \overbrace{\lambda x.\,\pi_2(f(x))}^\varepsilon
                    }
                \bigr)\\
            &\equiv
                \lambda x.\,\dep{\pi_1(f(x)),\pi_2(f(x))}.
    \end{align*}
    By Corollary~\ref{cor:Sigma-uniqueness}, for each $x\colon X$, the type
    \[
        \dep{\pi_1(f(x)),\pi_2(f(x))}
        \eq{\sum_{y\colon A(x)}P(x,y)}f(x)
    \]
    is inhabited. Therefore, by function extensionality,
    \[
        \fun{ac}^{-1}(\fun{ac}(f))
            \eq{\prod_{x\colon X}\sum_{y\colon A(x)}P(x,y)}f.
    \]
\end{proof}

\begin{rem}
    It is worth noting that the axiom of function extensionality was used to prove that the map $\fun{ac}$ is an equivalence. However, no axiom was needed for the construction of the global choice function $g\colon\prod_{x\colon X}A(x)$.
\end{rem}

\begin{thm}\fe
    Let\/ $A,B,C\colon\univ$ be types. Then the map
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{curry}\colon((A\times B)\to C)&\to(A\to(B\to C))\\
        f&\mapsto\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,f(x,y)
    \end{align*}
    is an equivalence.
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    Consider the function
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{uncurry}\colon(A\to(B\to C))&\to((A\times B)\to C)\\
        g&\mapsto\lambda\omega.\,g(\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega)).
    \end{align*}
    Evaluating $\fun{curry}\circ\fun{uncurry}$ at $g$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{curry}(\fun{uncurry}(g))
            &\equiv
                \fun{curry}
                    \bigl(
                        \lambda \omega.\,g(\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega))
                    \bigr)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,
                \bigl(
                    \lambda \omega.\,g(\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega))
                \bigr)(x,y)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,g(\pi_1(x,y))(\pi_2(x,y))\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,g(x)(y)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,g(x)\\
            &\equiv g.
    \end{align*}
    Moreover, evaluating $\fun{uncurry}\circ\fun{curry}$ at $f$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{uncurry}(\fun{curry}(f))
            &\equiv
                \fun{uncurry}
                \bigl(
                    \lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,f(x,y)
                \bigr)\\
            &\equiv
                \lambda \omega.\,
                \bigl(
                    \lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,f(x,y)
                \bigr)
                    (\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega))\\
            &\equiv
                \lambda\omega.\,
                \bigl(
                    \lambda y.\,f(\pi_1(\omega)),y)
                \bigr)(\pi_2(\omega))\\
            &\equiv\lambda\omega.\,f(\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega)).
    \end{align*}
    By Corollary~\ref{cor:cartesian-uniqueness}, for each $\omega\colon A\times B$, there is a path $(\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega))\eq{A\times B}\omega$. Applying $\fun{ap}_f$, we obtain a path $f(\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega))\eq{C}f(\omega)$. Therefore, function extensionality implies that
    \[
        \fun{uncurry}(\fun{curry}(f))\eq{(A\times B)\to C}f,
    \]
    which completes the proof.
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}\fe
    Let\/ $A,B\colon\univ$ be types, and let\/ $C\colon A\times B\to\univ$ be a dependent type family. Then the map
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{curry}\colon\biggl(\prod_{\omega\colon A\times B}C(\omega)\biggr)&\to\prod_{x\colon A}\prod_{y\colon B}C((x,y))\\
        f&\mapsto\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,f((x,y))
    \end{align*}
    is an equivalence.
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    Consider the function\footnote{This is none other than the induction principle for products.}
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{uncurry}&\colon
            \biggl(
                \prod_{x\colon A}\;\prod_{y\colon B}C((x,y))
            \biggr)
            \to\prod_{\omega\colon A\times B}C(\omega)\\
        g(\omega)&\defeq\fun{transport}^C
            \bigl(
                \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega),
                g(\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega))
            \bigr).
    \end{align*}
    Evaluating $\fun{curry}\circ\fun{uncurry}$ at $g$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{curry}(\fun{uncurry}(g))
            &\equiv
            \fun{curry}
            \bigl(
                \lambda\omega.\,\fun{transport}^C
                    \bigl(
                        \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega),
                        g(\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega))
                    \bigr)
            \bigr)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,g(x)(y)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,g(x)\\
            &\equiv g,
    \end{align*}
    where the second definitional equality is justified by the definitional equality
    \[
        \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}((x,y))\equiv\fun{refl}_{(x,y)},
    \]
    which causes the transport to vanish.
    
    Moreover, evaluating $\fun{uncurry}\circ\fun{curry}$ at $f$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{uncurry}(\fun{curry}(f))
            &\equiv\fun{uncurry}
                \bigl(
                    \lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,f((x,y))
                \bigr)\\
            &\equiv\lambda\omega.\,\fun{transport}^C
                \bigl(
                    \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega),
                    f((\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega)))
                \bigr).
    \end{align*}
    By Lemma~\ref{lem:dependent-map}, the dependent action on paths
    \[
        \fun{apd}_f
            \bigl(
            (\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega)),
            \omega,
            \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega)\bigr)
    \]
    is a witness of the identity type
    \[
        \fun{transport}^C
            \bigl(
                \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega),
                f((\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega)))
            \bigr)
            \eq{C(\omega)}f(\omega).
    \]
    Hence, for each $\omega\colon A\times B$, there is a path
    \[
        \fun{uncurry}(\fun{curry}(f))(\omega)
        \eq{C(\omega)}
        f(\omega),
    \]
    and the result follows by function extensionality.
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}\fe\label{thm:universal-property-of-product-over-coproduct}
    Let\/ $A$ and\/ $B$ be types, and let\/ $C\colon A+B\to\univ$ be a type family. Then there is an equivalence
    \[
        \prod_{z\colon A+B}C(z)\simeq
            \Bigl
                (\prod_{x\colon A}C(\fun{inl}(x))
            \Bigr)
            \times
            \Bigl(
                \prod_{y\colon B}C(\fun{inr}(y))
            \Bigr).
    \]
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    The forward map is defined as
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{split}&\defeq\lambda f.\,(f\circ\fun{inl},f\circ\fun{inr}),
    \intertext{and the backward map as}
        \fun{merge}&\defeq\lambda(u,v).\,\fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,u,v).
    \end{align*}
    To complete the proof, we compute both compositions. For the first composition, we have:
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{split}(\fun{merge}(u,v))
            &\equiv\fun{split}(\fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,u,v))\\
            &\equiv(\fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,u,v)\circ\fun{inl},\fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,u,v)\circ\fun{inr}).
    \end{align*}
    Evaluating these precompositions at $x\colon A$ and $y\colon B$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,u,v,\fun{inl}(x))&\equiv u(x),\\
        \fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,u,v,\fun{inr}(y))&\equiv v(y).
    \end{align*}
    Thus, the composition is equal to the identity function.

    For the other composition, we have:
    \[
        \fun{merge}(\fun{split}(f))
            \equiv\fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,f\circ\fun{inl},f\circ\fun{inr}),
    \]
    which, by function extensionality, equals $f$ because both functions yield the same results when precomposed with $\fun{inl}$ and $\fun{inr}$.
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}\fe
    Let\/ $A\colon\univ$ be a type and $B\colon A\to\univ$ a type family. Given a dependent type family\/ $C\colon\Sigma_AB\to\univ$ over the total space $\Sigma_AB$, the map
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{curry}\colon
            \biggl(
                \prod_{\omega\colon\Sigma_AB}C(\omega)
            \biggr)
            &\to
            \prod_{x\colon A}\;\prod_{y\colon B(x)}C((x,y))\\
        f&\mapsto\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,f((x,y))
    \end{align*}
    is an equivalence.
\end{thm}

\begin{proof}
    Consider the function\footnote{This is the induction principle for dependent pairs.}
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{uncurry}
            &\colon
                \biggl(
                    \prod_{x\colon A}\prod_{y\colon B(x)}C((x,y))
                \biggr)
            \to\prod_{\omega\colon\Sigma_AB}C(\omega)\\
            \fun{uncurry}(g)
                &\defeq\lambda\omega.\,\fun{transport}^C
                    \bigl(
                        \fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}(\omega),
                        g(\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega))
                    \bigr),
    \end{align*}
    where the map
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}\colon\prod_{\omega\colon\Sigma_AB}(\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega))\eq{\Sigma_AB}\omega
    \end{align*}
    is the uniqueness principle defined after Corollary~\ref{cor:Sigma-uniqueness}.

    Evaluating\/ $\fun{curry}\circ\fun{uncurry}$ at\/ $g$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{curry}&(\fun{uncurry}(g))
            \equiv\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,\fun{uncurry}(g)((x,y))\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,\fun{transport}^C\bigl(\fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}((x,y)),g(\pi_1((x,y)))(\pi_2((x,y)))\bigr)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,g(x)(y)\\
            &\equiv\lambda x.\,g(x)\\
            &\equiv g,
    \end{align*}
    where the third definitional equality is justified by the fact that
    \[
        \fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}((x,y))\equiv\fun{refl}_{(x,y)},
    \]
    which causes the transport to vanish.

    Moreover, evaluating\/ $\fun{uncurry}\circ\fun{curry}$ at\/ $f$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{uncurry}(\fun{curry}(f))
            &\equiv\fun{uncurry}\bigl(\lambda x.\,\lambda y.\,f((x,y))\bigr)\\
            &\equiv\lambda\omega.\,\fun{transport}^C\bigl(\fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}(\omega),f((\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega)))\bigr).
    \end{align*}
    By Lemma~\ref{lem:dependent-map}, the dependent action on paths
    \[
        \fun{apd}_f
            (
                \pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega),
                \fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}(\omega)
            )
    \]
    is a witness of the identity type
    \[
        \fun{transport}^C\bigl(\fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}(\omega),f((\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega)))\bigr)\eq{C(\omega)}f(\omega).
    \]
    Hence, for each\/ $\omega\colon\Sigma_AB$, there is a path
    \[
        \fun{uncurry}(\fun{curry}(f))(\omega)\eq{C(\omega)}f(\omega),
    \]
    and the result follows by function extensionality.
\end{proof}

\section{Exercises}

\begin{exr}
    Derive the induction principle for products\/ $\fun{ind}_{A\times B}$, using only the projections and the propositional uniqueness principle\/ $\fun{uniq}_{A\times B}$. Verify that the definitional equalities are valid. Generalize\/ $\fun{uniq}_{A\times B}$ to\/ $\Sigma$-types, and do the same for\/ $\Sigma$-types.
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    Let $C\colon A\times B\to\univ$ be a type family. We have to define a function
    \[
        \fun{ind}_{A\times B}\colon
            \biggl(
                \prod_{x\colon A}\;\prod_{y\colon B}C((x,y))
            \biggr)
            \to\prod_{\omega\colon A\times B}C(\omega)
    \]
    such that
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:base-1}
        \fun{ind}_{A\times B}(f)((x,y))\equiv f(x)(y).
    \end{equation}
    To achieve this, we define
    \[
        \fun{ind}_{A\times B}(f)\defeq\lambda\omega.\,
            \fun{transport}^C
            \bigl(
                \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega),
                f(\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega))
            \bigr).
    \]
    Since
    \[
        \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega)\colon
            (\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega))
            \eq{A\times B}
            \omega,
    \]
    we have
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{transport}^C
            &\bigl(
                \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega)
            \bigr)\colon
                C((\pi_1(\omega),\pi_2(\omega)))
            \to C(\omega)\\
        \fun{transport}^C
            &\bigl(
                \fun{uniq}_{A\times B}(\omega),
                f(\pi_1(\omega))(\pi_2(\omega))
            \bigr)
            \colon C(\omega),
    \end{align*}
    which shows that $\fun{ind}_{A\times B}$ is well defined.
    
    Moreover, given that $\fun{uniq}_{A\times B}((x,y))\equiv\fun{refl}_{(x,y)}$, the transport vanishes and equation~\eqref{eq:base-1} is satisfied because $\pi_1((x,y))\equiv x$ and $\pi_2((x,y))\equiv y$.

    Let us now do the same for $\Sigma_AB$, where $B\colon A\to\univ$ is a type family. We have to define
    \[
        \fun{ind}_{\Sigma_AB}\colon
            \biggl(
                \prod_{x\colon A}\;\prod_{y\colon B(x)}C((x,y))
            \biggr)
            \to
            \prod_{\zeta\colon\Sigma_AB}C(\zeta)
    \]
    such that
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:base-2}
        \fun{ind}_{\Sigma_AB}(f)((x,y))\equiv f(x)(y).
    \end{equation}
    To fulfill the requirement, we would like to define the function along the lines of
    \[
        \fun{ind}_{\Sigma_AB}(f)
            \defeq\lambda\zeta.\,
            \fun{transport}^C
                \bigl(
                    \fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}(\zeta),
                    f(\pi_1(\zeta))(\pi_2(\zeta))
                \bigr).
    \]
    This shows that we first need to assume the existence of a map
    \[
        \fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}
            \colon\prod_{\zeta\colon\Sigma_AB}
            (\pi_1(\zeta),\pi_2(\zeta))
            \eq{\Sigma_AB}\zeta
    \]
    satisfying $\fun{uniq}_{\Sigma_AB}((a,b))\equiv\fun{refl}_{(a,b)}$ for all $a\colon A$ and all $b\colon B(a)$.
    
    In sum, this exercise shows that $\fun{ind}$ and $\fun{uniq}$ are deducible from each other for both dependent and non-dependent products.
    %
    
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}\fe
    Show that if we define\/ $A\times B\defeq\prod_{z\colon\fun2}\fun{rec}_{\fun2}(\univ,A,B,z)$, then we can give a definition of\/ $\fun{ind}_{A\times' B}$ for which the definitional equalities stated in\/ {\upshape\nref{lpar:induction}} hold propositionally, i.e., using equality types.
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    Let
    \[
        A\times'B\defeq\prod_{z\colon\fun2}
            \fun{rec}_{\fun2}(\univ,A,B,z),
    \]
    where $\fun{rec}_{\fun2}(\univ)$ is defined in \nref{lpar:boolean-type} as
    \[
        \fun{rec}_{\fun2}(\univ)\colon
            \univ\to\univ\to\fun2\to\univ.
    \]
    Thus, the elements of $A\times'B$ are dependent functions $\omega$ that satisfy
    \begin{align*}
        \omega(0_{\fun2})&\colon A\\
        \omega(1_{\fun2})&\colon B.
    \end{align*}
    Before proceeding, let us establish that the canonical elements of $A\times'B$ are the dependent functions $\varepsilon_{(a,b)}$ given by pairs $(a,b)\colon A\times B$, such that $\varepsilon_{(a,b)}(0_{\fun2})\equiv a$ and $\varepsilon_{(a,b)}(1_{\fun2})\equiv b$. Under this convention, for $C\colon A\times'B\to\univ$, we have to define
    \begin{align*}
        \fun{ind}_{A\times'B}&\colon
            \biggl(
                \prod_{x\colon A}\;\prod_{y\colon B}
                    C(\varepsilon_{(x,y)})
            \biggr)
            \to\prod_{\omega\colon A\times'B}C(\omega).
    \end{align*}
    Let $f$ be a function in the domain. Then, given a pair $(x,y)$ in the official product $A\times B$, we have
    \[
        f(x)(y)\colon C(\varepsilon_{(x,y)}).
    \]
    We want to produce from this a function $\hat f$ such that, for every
    \[
        \omega\colon\prod_{z\colon\fun2}
            \fun{rec}_{\fun2}(\univ,A,B,z),
    \]
    we obtain $\hat f(\omega)\colon C(\omega)$. In addition, we require that this function satisfy
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:propositional-base-condition}
        \hat f(\varepsilon_{(a,b)})
        \eq{C(\varepsilon_{(a,b)})}
        f(a)(b).
    \end{equation}
    Before defining $\hat f(\omega)$, let us take into account that 
    \[
        f(\omega(0_{\fun2}))(\omega(1_{\fun2}))
            \colon C(
                \varepsilon_{
                    (
                        \omega(0_{\fun2}),\omega(1_{\fun2})
                    )
                }
                ).
    \]
    For $\omega\colon A\times'B$, introduce
    \[
        E_\omega\defeq\lambda z\colon\fun2.\,
            \varepsilon_{
                (\omega(0_{\fun2}),\omega(1_{\fun2}))
            }(z)
            \eq{\fun{rec}_{\fun2}(\univ,A,B,z)}
            \omega(z).
    \]
    Using the induction principle of booleans, to define a dependent function of this type it is enough to provide two constants $e_0\colon E_\omega(0_{\fun2})$ and $e_1\colon E_\omega(1_{\fun2})$. Since this condition is fulfilled by $e_0\defeq\fun{refl}_{\omega(0_{\fun2})}$ and $e_1\defeq\fun{refl}_{\omega(1_{\fun2})}$, we conclude that there is a dependent function
    \[
        e_\omega\defeq\fun{ind}_{\fun2}(E_\omega,
            \fun{refl}_{\omega(0_{\fun2})},
            \fun{refl}_{\omega(1_{\fun2})})
            \colon\prod_{z\colon\fun2}E_\omega(z).
    \]
    By function extensionality, for $\hat e_\omega\defeq\fun{funext}(e_\omega)$, we deduce that
    \[
        \hat e_\omega\colon
            \varepsilon_{
                (\omega(0_{\fun2}),\omega(1_{\fun2}))
            }
            \eq{A\times'B}
            \omega.
    \]
    In particular,
    \begin{equation}\label{eq:proposition-base-condition-2}
        \hat e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}\colon
            \varepsilon_{(a,b)}
            \eq{A\times'B}
            \varepsilon_{(a,b)}.
    \end{equation}
    Therefore, we can define
    \[
        \hat f(\omega)\defeq\fun{transport}^C
            \bigl(
                \hat e_\omega,
                f(\omega(0_{\fun2}))(\omega(1_{\fun2}))
            \bigr),
    \]
    which inhabits $C(\omega)$.
    
    To verify condition~\eqref{eq:propositional-base-condition}, observe that by boolean induction on the constants, we have
    \begin{align*}
        e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}(0_{\fun2})
            &\equiv\fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}(0_{\fun2})}\\
        e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}(1_{\fun2})
            &\equiv\fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}(1_{\fun2})}.
    \end{align*}
    By induction on $z\colon\fun2$, we deduce a pointwise propositional equality
    \[
        H(z)\colon e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}(z)
        \eq{E_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}(z)}
        \fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}(z)}.
    \]
    By function extensionality, this implies an equality of dependent functions
    \[
        p\colon e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}
        \eq{\prod_{z\colon\fun2}E_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}(z)}
        \lambda z.\,\fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}(z)}.
    \]
    Applying $\fun{ap}_{\fun{funext}}$ to $p$, we obtain
    \[
        \fun{funext}(e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}})
        \eq{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}\eq{A\times'B}\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}
        \fun{funext}(\lambda z.\,\fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}(z)}).
    \]
    Since $\fun{funext}$ maps the constant reflexivity homotopy to the reflexivity path, we have $\fun{funext}(\lambda z.\,\fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}(z)})\equiv\fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}$. Thus, we have established a path
    \[
        q\colon\hat e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}
        \eq{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}\eq{A\times'B}\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}
        \fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}},
    \]
    where $\hat e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}}\defeq\fun{funext}(e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}})$.
    
    For $r$ in this identity type, define
    \[
        T_{(a,b)}(r)\defeq\fun{transport}^C
            \bigl(
                r,f(a)(b)
            \bigr).
    \]
    Applying $\fun{ap}_{T_{(a,b)}}$ to the path $q$, we obtain
    \begin{align*}
        \hat f(\varepsilon_{(a,b)})
            &\equiv\fun{transport}^C
                \bigl(
                    \hat e_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}},
                    f(a)(b)
                \bigr)\\
            &\eq{C(\varepsilon_{(a,b)})}
                \fun{transport}^C
                \bigl(
                    \fun{refl}_{\varepsilon_{(a,b)}},
                    f(a)(b)
                \bigr)\\
            &\equiv f(a)(b).
    \end{align*}
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}
    Define, by induction on\/ $n$, a general notion of\/ $n$-dimensional path in a type\/ $A$, simultaneously with the type of boundaries for such paths.

    {\upshape Hint: If\/ $B\colon\N\to\univ$ denotes the type family of boundary types and\/ $P\colon\N\to\univ$ the family of path types, then\/ $P(n)\colon B(n)\to\univ$.}
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    Let us begin by saying that the boundaries of a path $p\colon x\eq Ay$ are the terms $x$ and $y$.

    With this in mind, what we need to do is to define, for every $n\colon\N$, two types $P(n)$ and $B(n)$, the first one representing $n$-paths and the second $n$-boundaries.

    In this regard, $P$ and $B$ will be type families with signature $\N\to\univ$, whose definitions will follow from induction on $n$, while satisfying
    \[
        P(n)\colon B(n)\to\univ.
    \]
    This means that we have to define two initial types $P(0)$ and $B(0)$, and two step functions. Since we cannot simply define $B(\fun{succ}(n))$ as $P(n)\times P(n)$ because we must ensure that both $n$-paths share the same ends, the actual definition is more involved. The idea comes after considering the first few values of $n$:
    \begin{align*}
        &\begin{cases}
            B(0)
           \defeq\fun1\\
        P(0)
            \defeq\lambda u.\,A.
        \end{cases}\\
        &\begin{cases}
            B(1)
            \defeq\sum_{z\colon B(0)}P(0)(z)\times P(0)(z)\\
            P(1)
                \defeq\lambda(z,(a_1,a_2)).\,
                a_1\eq{P(0)(z)}a_2.
        \end{cases}\\
        &\begin{cases}
            B(2)
            \defeq\sum_{z\colon B(1)}P(1)(z)\times P(1)(z)\\
            P(2)\defeq\lambda(z,(p_1,p_2)).\,
                p_1\eq{P(1)(z)}p_2.
        \end{cases}
            \intertext{In general,}
        &\begin{cases}
            B(\fun{succ}(n))
                \defeq\sum_{z\colon B(n)}P(n)(z)\times P(n)(z)\\
            P(\fun{succ}(n))
                \defeq\lambda(z,(\alpha_1,\alpha_2)).\,
                    \alpha_1\eq{P(n)(z)}\alpha_2.
        \end{cases}
    \end{align*}
    To define a function
    \[
        c\colon\N\to\Bigl(\sum_{B\colon\univ}B\to\univ\Bigr)
    \]
    by recursion on $n$, we specify
    \begin{align*}
        c_0&\defeq\dep{\fun1,\lambda z.\,A}\\
        c_s&\colon\N
            \to\Bigl(\sum_{B\colon\univ}B\to\univ\Bigr)
            \to\Bigl(\sum_{B\colon\univ}B\to\univ\Bigr)\\
        c_s&\defeq\lambda n.\,
            \lambda\dep{B,h}.\,
            \Bigdep{
                \Bigl(\sum_{z\colon B}h(z)\times h(z)\Bigr),
                \lambda(\dep{z,(h_1,h_2)}).\,h_1\eq{h(z)}h_2
            }.
    \end{align*}
    Note that, by abuse of notation, we have written $\lambda\dep{B,h}$ instead of $\lambda x$ and $B$ and $h$ instead of $\pi_1(x)$ and $\pi_2(x)$.
    
    We can now define
    \begin{align*}
        c(n)&\defeq\fun{rec}_\N
            \Bigl(
                \sum_{B\colon\univ}B\to\univ,
                c_0,
                c_s,
                n
            \Bigr)
    \end{align*}
    and with this
    \[
        B(n)\defeq\pi_1(c(n))
        \quad\text{and}\quad
        P(n)\defeq\pi_2(c(n)).
    \]
    Since
    \begin{align*}
        \dep{B(\fun{succ}(n)),P(\fun{succ}(n))}
            &\equiv c(\fun{succ}(n))\\
            &\equiv c_s(n,c(n))\\
            &\equiv c_s(n,\dep{B(n),P(n)}),
    \end{align*}
    i.e.,
    \begin{align*}
        B(\fun{succ}(n))
            &\equiv\sum_{z\colon B(n)}
                P(n)(z)\times P(n)(z)\\
        P(\fun{succ}(n))&\equiv
            \lambda\dep{z,(\alpha_1,\alpha_2)}.\,
                \alpha_1\eq{P(n)(z)}\alpha_2,
    \end{align*}
    as desired.
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}
    For any\/ $x, y\colon A$, a path\/ $p\colon x\eq{A}y$, and a function\/ $f\colon A\to B$, show that by concatenating with\/ $\fun{transportconst}^B_p(f(x))$ and its inverse, respectively, we obtain functions
    \[
        (f(x)\eq{B}f(y))\to(p_*(f(x))\eq{B}f(y))
    \]
    and
    \[
        (p_*(f(x))\eq{B}f(y))\to(f(x)\eq{B}f(y)).
    \]
    Prove that these functions are in fact inverse equivalences.
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    Given that
    \[
        \fun{transportconst}^B_p(f(x))\colon p_*(f(x))
            \eq Bf(x),
    \]
    we can define
    \begin{align*}
        \phi\colon(f(x)\eq Bf(y))&\to(p_*(f(x))\eq Bf(y))\\
            q&\mapsto\fun{transportconst}^B_p(f(x))
                \ct q
    \intertext{and}
        \psi\colon(p_*(f(x))\eq Bf(y))&\to(f(x)\eq Bf(y))\\
        r&\mapsto\fun{transportconst}^B_p(f(x))^{-1}
            \ct r.
    \end{align*}
    To shorten the notation let us introduce
    \[
        T(x)\defeq\fun{transportconst}^B_p(f(x)).
    \]
    Then,
    \begin{align*}
        \phi(\psi(r))&\equiv T(x)\ct(T(x)^{-1}\ct r)\\
            &\eq{p_*(f(x))\eq{B}f(y)}(T(x)\ct T(x)^{-1})\ct r\\
            &\eq{p_*(f(x))\eq{B}f(y)}
                \fun{refl}_{p_*(f(x))}\ct r\\
            &\equiv r.
    \intertext{and}
        \psi(\phi(q))&\equiv T(x)^{-1}\ct(T(x)\ct q)\\
            &\eq{f(x)\eq Bf(y)}(T(x)^{-1}\ct T(x))\ct q\\
            &\eq{f(x)\eq Bf(y)}\fun{refl}_{f(x)}\ct q\\
            &\equiv q.
    \end{align*}
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}
    Let\/ $A$ be a type and let\/ $x, y, z\colon A$ with a path\/ $p\colon x\eq{A}y$. Show that the function\/ $(p\ct\dummy)\colon(y\eq{A}z)\to(x\eq{A}z)$ is an equivalence.
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    Consider the type family $E(w)\defeq w\eq{A}z$. By Lemma~\ref{lem:path-equivalences} b) applied to the case $a\equiv z$ and $p^{-1}\colon y\eq Ax$, we know that
    \begin{align*}
        (y\eq{A}z)&\to(x\eq{A}z)\\
        q&\mapsto p\ct q
    \end{align*}
    is an equivalence.
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}
    State and prove a generalization of\/ {\upshape Theorem~\ref{thm:pair-fxg}} from cartesian products to $\Sigma$-types.
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    See Theorem~\ref{thm:pair-dependent-gxh}.
\end{solution}


\begin{exr}
    State and prove the analogue of\/ {\upshape Theorem~\ref{thm:pair-fxg}} for coproducts.
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    See Theorem \ref{thm:pair-f+g}.
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}\fe
    Prove that coproducts have the expected universal property, 
    \[
        ((A+B)\to X)\simeq(A\to X)\times(B\to X).
    \]
    Can you generalize this to an equivalence involving dependent functions?
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    Define
    \begin{align*}
        \varphi\colon(A+B\to X)&\to((A\to X)\times(B\to X))\\
        f&\mapsto(f\circ\fun{inl},f\circ\fun{inr}).
    \end{align*}
    For its quasi-inverse, consider
    \begin{align*}
        \psi\colon((A\to X)\times(B\to X))&\to(A+B\to X)\\
        (u,v)&\mapsto\fun{rec}_{A+B}(X,u,v).
    \end{align*}
    For the first composition, the definition of $\fun{rec}_{A+B}$ yields
    \begin{align*}
        \varphi(\psi(u,v))&\equiv
            \big(
                \fun{rec}_{A+B}(X,u,v)\circ\fun{inl},
                \fun{rec}_{A+B}(X,u,v)\circ\fun{inr}
            \big)\\
            &\equiv(u,v).
    \end{align*}
    For the second composition, we have
    \begin{align*}
        \psi(\varphi(f))
            &\equiv\fun{rec}_{A+B}
                (X,f\circ\fun{inl},f\circ\fun{inr}).
    \intertext{From the definition of $\fun{rec}_{A+B}$, we obtain}
        \psi(\varphi(f))(\fun{inl}(a))
            &\equiv f(\fun{inl}(a)),\\
        \psi(\varphi(f))(\fun{inr}(b))
            &\equiv f(\fun{inr}(b)),
    \intertext{for all $a\colon A$ and $b\colon B$. By function extensionality, this pointwise equality constructs paths}
        (\psi(\varphi(f)))\circ\fun{inl}
            &\eq{A\to X}f\circ\fun{inl},\\
        (\psi(\varphi(f)))\circ\fun{inr}
            &\eq{B\to X}f\circ\fun{inr}.
    \end{align*}
    Finally, from the induction principle of $A+B$, we conclude
    \[
        \psi(\varphi(f))\eq{A+B\to X}f.
    \]

    \textbf{Generalization.} For dependent functions the previous equivalence becomes
    \[
        \prod_{s\colon A+B}C(s)
        \simeq
        \Bigl(\prod_{a\colon A}C(\fun{inl}(a))\Bigr)
        \times
        \Bigl(\prod_{b\colon B}C(\fun{inlr}(b))\Bigr).
    \]
    Define
    \begin{align*}
        \varphi\colon\prod_{s\colon A+B}C(s)
        &\to
        \Bigl(\prod_{a\colon A}C(\fun{inl}(a))\Bigr)
        \times
        \Bigl(\prod_{b\colon B}C(\fun{inlr}(b))\Bigr)\\
        f&\mapsto(f\circ\fun{inl},f\circ\fun{inr}).
    \end{align*}
    For the quasi-inverse, consider
    \begin{align*}
        \psi\colon\Bigl(\prod_{a\colon A}C(\fun{inl}(a))\Bigr)
        \times
        \Bigl(\prod_{b\colon B}C(\fun{inr}(b))\Bigr)
        &\to
        \prod_{s\colon A+B}C(s)\\
        (u,v)&\mapsto\fun{ind}_{A+B}(C,u,v).
    \end{align*}
    The proofs of $\varphi\circ\psi\sim\id$ and $\psi\circ\varphi\sim\id$ are analogous to the previous case.
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}\label{exr:Sigma-associativity}
    Prove that\/ $\Sigma$-types are ``associative'', in that for any\/ $A\colon\univ$ and families\/ $B\colon A\to\univ$ and\/ $C\colon\Sigma_A B\to\univ$, we have
    \[
        \sum_{x\colon A}\;\sum_{y\colon B(x)}C(x,y)
        \simeq
        \sum_{p\colon\Sigma_A B}C(p).
    \]
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    See Theorem~\ref{thm:composite-transport}.
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}\label{exr:pullback}
    Formulate the universal property of a pullback square as an equivalence of types in Homotopy Type Theory.
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    Recall the universal property of the pullback captured by the following commutative diagram:
    \[
        \begin{tikzcd}
            X
                    \arrow[rdd,bend right,"u"']
                    \arrow[rrd,bend left,"v"]
                    \arrow[rd,"\exists!h",dashed]\\
                &P
                    \arrow[r,"\pi_V"]
                    \arrow[d,"\pi_U"']
                    \arrow[rd,
                        "\lrcorner",
                        phantom,
                        very near start]
                &V
                    \arrow[d,"g_V"]\\
                &U
                    \arrow[r,"g_U"']
                &Q
        \end{tikzcd}
    \]
    In the context of Homotopy Type Theory, however, trying to translate the categorical definition word for word leads to a complex setting of conditions involving $1$-, $2$-, and $3$-paths.

    A better way to address the problem is to express the universal property as an equivalence between types.

    To define those types, fix the functions
    \[
        U\xrightarrow{g_U}Q\xleftarrow{g_V}V
    \]
    and observe that a square subtended by $(u,v)\colon X\to U\times V$ is commutative when the type
    \[
        W(u,v)\defeq g_U\circ u
            \eq{X\to Q}
            g_V\circ v
    \]
    is inhabited. Thus, the type of commutative squares is
    \[
        \Sigma(X)\defeq\sum_{(u,v)\colon X\to(U\times V)}
            W(u,v),
    \]
    where we have identified $X\to(U\times V)$ with $(X\to U)\times(X\to V)$.

    On the other hand, the type
    \[
        X\to P
    \]
    represents the type of functions $h$ such that the square induced by $h$ via precomposition with $\dep{(\pi_U,\pi_V),p}\colon \Sigma(P)$ is a pullback.

    Therefore, we can define a canonical map
    \begin{align*}
        \varphi\colon (X\to P)&\to \Sigma(X)\\
        h &\mapsto
            \bigdep{
                (\pi_U\circ h,\pi_V\circ h),
                p\rwct h
            },
    \end{align*}
    where $p\rwct h$ denotes right whiskering.\footnote{As defined in \nameref{naturality-of-funcext}.}
    
    \textsl{Definition.} A commutative square $\dep{(\pi_U,\pi_V),p}\colon \Sigma(P)$ is a \textsl{pullback} if the canonical map $\varphi$ is an equivalence for every type $X\colon\univ$.

    Suppose that $\dep{(\pi_U,\pi_V),p}$ is a pullback. Since $\varphi$ is an embedding, from equality in Cartesian products and in $\Sigma$-types, we obtain
    \begin{equation*}
        \left.\begin{aligned}
            q_U&\colon\pi_U\circ h'\eq{X\to U}\pi_U\circ h\\
            q_V&\colon\pi_V\circ h'\eq{X\to V}\pi_V\circ h\\
            \tau_{UV}&\colon\fun{transport}^W
                \big(
                    q_{UV},
                    p\rwct h'
                \big)
                \eq{W(\pi_U\circ h,\pi_V\circ h)}p\rwct h
        \end{aligned}\right\}
        \implies h'\eq{X\to P}h,
    \end{equation*}
    where $q_{UV}\defeq\fun{pair}^=(q_U,q_V)$. By Proposition~\ref{prop:transport-in-commutative-squares}, the path $\tau_{UV}$ computes to
    \[
        \tau_{UV}\colon
            (g_U\lwct q_U)^{-1}
            \ct(p\rwct h')
            \ct (g_V \lwct q_V)
            \eq{W(\pi_U \circ h, \pi_V \circ h)}p\rwct h,
    \]
    or equivalently (recycling the notation)
    \[
        \tau_{UV}\colon
            (p\rwct h')\ct(g_V\lwct q_V)
            \eq{W(\pi_U\circ h',\pi_V\circ h)}
            (g_U\lwct q_U)\ct(p\rwct h).
    \]
    To verify this last equality, note that the \lhs starts at $g_U\circ\pi_U\circ h'$ and ends at $g_V\circ v$. The \rhs also starts at $g_U\circ\pi_U\circ h'$ and ends at $g_V\circ v$. Therefore, the equality is between two paths of type $g_U\circ\pi_U\circ h'\eq{X\to Q}g_V\circ v$, which corresponds exactly to the definition of $W(\pi_U\circ h',v)$.
    
    On the other hand, considering a quasi-inverse map
    \[
        \varphi^{-1}\colon \Sigma(X)\to(X\to P),
    \]
    for each square $s\defeq\dep{(u,v),q}\colon\Sigma(X)$, we obtain a function $h\colon X\to P$ and a homotopy
    \[
        \varepsilon\colon\varphi(h)\eq{\Sigma(X)}s.
    \]
    By definition of the canonical map, this equality expands to
    \[
        \varepsilon\colon\dep{(\pi_U\circ h,\pi_V\circ h), p\rwct h}
            \eq{\Sigma(X)}
            \dep{(u,v),q}.
    \]
    Unpacking this equality in the $\Sigma$-type $\Sigma(X)$ yields:
    \begin{align*}
        r_U&\colon\pi_U\circ h\eq{X\to U}u,\\
        r_V&\colon\pi_V\circ h\eq{X\to V}v,\\
        \tau&\colon\fun{transport}^W
            \big(
                r_{UV},
                p\rwct h
            \big)
            \eq{W(u,v)}q,
        \end{align*}
    where $r_{UV}\defeq\fun{pair}^=(r_U,r_V)$.

    Proposition~\ref{prop:transport-in-commutative-squares} applied to this case computes the transport function above as
    \[
        \fun{transport}^W(r_{UV},p\rwct h)\eq{W(u,v)}
            (g_U\lwct r_U)^{-1}\ct(p\rwct h)\ct(g_V\lwct r_V),
    \]
    and so we can express the third equality as
    \[
        \tau\colon(g_U\lwct r_U^{-1})\ct(p\rwct h)\ct(g_V\lwct r_V)
        \eq{W(u,v)}q,
    \]
    by Proposition~\ref{prop:function-path-whiskering-properties}.
    
\end{solution}

\begin{exr}
    Suppose we are given two commutative squares
    \[
        \begin{tikzcd}
            A
                    \arrow[r]
                    \arrow[d]
                &C
                    \arrow[r]
                    \arrow[d]
                    \arrow[dr,
                        phantom,
                        "\lrcorner",
                        very near start]
                &E
                    \arrow[d]\\
            B
                    \arrow[r]
                &D
                    \arrow[r]
                &F
        \end{tikzcd}
    \]
    and suppose that the right-hand square is a pullback. Prove that the left-hand square is a pullback square if, and only if, the outer rectangle is a pullback.
\end{exr}

\begin{solution}
    Here we adopt the function names shown in the following commutative diagram:
    \[
        \begin{tikzcd}
        A
                \arrow[r,"\pi_C"]
                \arrow[d,"\pi_B"']
            &C
                \arrow[r,"\pi_E"]
                \arrow[d,"\pi_D"']
                \arrow[rd,
                    "\lrcorner",
                    phantom,
                    very near start]
            &E
                \arrow[d,"g_E"]\\
        B
                \arrow[r,"g_B"']
            &D
                \arrow[r,"g_D"']
            &F
        \end{tikzcd}
    \]
    where the commutativity of both squares is witnessed by
    \[
        p_L\colon g_B\circ\pi_B\eq{A\to D}\pi_D\circ\pi_C
        \quad\text{and}\quad
        p_R\colon g_D\circ\pi_D\eq{C\to F}g_E\circ\pi_E.
    \]
    We add subscripts $R$ and $L$ to the notation of the previous exercise ($\varphi$, $W$, and $\Sigma$) to adapt it to the right and left squares, while dropping them for the outer rectangle. Thus, our setting defines
    \begin{align*}
        \varphi_R\colon(X\to C)&\to\Sigma_R(X)\\
        h&\mapsto
            \dep{
                (\pi_D\circ h,\pi_E\circ h),
                p_R\rwct h
            },\\
        \varphi_L\colon (X\to A)&\to\Sigma_L(X)\\
        h&\mapsto
            \dep{
                (\pi_B\circ h,\pi_C\circ h),
                p_L\rwct h
            },\\
        \varphi\colon(X\to A)&\to\Sigma(X)\\
        h&\mapsto
            \dep{
                (\pi_B\circ h,\pi_E\circ\pi_C\circ h),
                p\rwct h
            }.
    \end{align*}
    The problem statement becomes:
    \begin{quote}\itshape
        If $\varphi_R$ is an equivalence, $\varphi_L$ is an equivalence if, and only if, $\varphi$ is.
    \end{quote}

    We will establish an equivalence
    \[
        \psi\colon\Sigma(X)\to\Sigma_L(X).
    \]
    Let $\dep{(u,v),q}\colon\Sigma(X)$. Since $\dep{(g_B\circ u,v),q}\colon\Sigma_R(X)$ and $\varphi_R$ is an equivalence, there exists a function $h_R\colon X\to C$, namely $\varphi_R^{-1}\dep{(g_B\circ u,v),q}$, such that
    \[
        \varphi_R(h_R)\eq{\Sigma_R(X)}\dep{(g_B\circ u,v),q},
    \]
    In particular, the first component of the equality is a path $r_D$ that witnesses the commutativity of the shaded subdiagram
    \[
        r_D\colon \pi_D\circ h_R\eq{X\to D}g_B\circ u
    \qquad
        \begin{tikzcd}[
            execute at end picture=
                {
                \fill[blue,opacity=0.1]
                  (\tikzcdmatrixname-1-1.center) to[bend right]
                  (\tikzcdmatrixname-3-2.center) --
                  (\tikzcdmatrixname-3-3.center) --
                  (\tikzcdmatrixname-2-3.center) to[bend right] cycle;
                }]
            X
